            }
            return "blank";

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1e493f9f00ac4935868ef72ea87b165a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '15df4dd3-71df-4f63-8f0a-f5aabf7cbdad'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d190701642" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>High theoretical capacity, stabilized and layered 2H phase molybdenum disulfide (2H) is regarded as a promising material for superior kinetics anode in sodium ion battery, but the inherent semiconducting properties and sluggish sodium ions diffusion limit the development of 2H based anode. Hence, this study builds a unique metalloid phase with high electronic conductivity and suitable layer structure by screening non-metallic carbon atoms doped in 2H (2H-C). As experimental and simulation results, 2H-C of metalloid properties after carbon atom modulation exhibit superior sodium storage kinetics and reversibility by a unique mechanism that promotes ion intercalation under high voltage window, and further good for the later uniformly conversion at lower voltage (intercalation-conversion mechanism). The 2H-C anode material delivers a remarkable reversible capacity of 156 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at ultra-high 100.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, long-term life of 6000 cycles, and superior performance at low temperatures of −10 °C. Otherwise, 2H-C/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell also performs charge/discharge capacity of 80.0/76.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with 87% capacity retention after 140 cycles at 0 °C. This discovery further explores the unique mechanism and application of 2D layered materials in sodium-ion battery anodes for fast kinetics under extreme conditions.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400470-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400470-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received great attention due to their wider temperature operating range, abundant crustal reserves,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0001_R_d190701634e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and high safety compared to lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0002_R_d190701634e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Small. J. Energy Chem.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the diffusion rate of sodium ions in the active anode material and the surface charge transfer resistance could become slow under extreme state,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0003_R_d190701634e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> hindering the development of low-temperature and fast-charging SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0004_R_d190701634e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, it is urgent to develop fast kinetics SIBs. The current mature studies are mainly based on electrolyte optimization of SEI to promote charge transfer, which leads to fast kinetic behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0005_R_d190701634e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Int. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The development of active anode materials suitable for superior kinetics still needs to be explored.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0006_R_d190701634e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the large size of sodium ions, the charge/discharge process is not favorable for Na ions de-embedding. The complex structure of amorphous carbon materials also leads to uncertainty in the sodium storage mechanism, which results in conventional commercial carbon-based anodes hindering the development of kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0007_R_d190701634e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the problem of high charge transfer resistance at low temperatures or fast charging hinders kinetic energy conversion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0008_R_d190701634e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the exploration of SIBs with good electrochemical properties and fast kinetics is a necessary step toward their large-scale application, especially at extreme state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0009_R_d190701634e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And of the anode candidates other than carbon, 2D materials with lamellar structures weakly bonded by van der Waals forces are becoming known, which provide more storage sites and diffusion pathways for Na ions and facilitates the processes of sodiation/desodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0010_R_d190701634e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MoS<sub>2</sub> is an ideal anode material that have a similar layer structure of graphite,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0011_R_d190701634e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> that provides useful internal space for Na ions intercalation and diffusion for superior ion storage kinetics. Additionally, MoS<sub>2</sub> could react with Na and reversibly convert to Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S<i><sub>x</sub></i>, which could provide higher theoretical capacity (670&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0012_R_d190701634e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy. J. Mater. Sci. Technol.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>However, the MoS<sub>2</sub> lattice structure can be divided into a stable 2H semiconductor phase and an unstable 1T metal phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0013_R_d190701634e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Energy Storage.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 1T metallic MoS<sub>2</sub> has high electrical conductivity and accelerate the ion transform, but its natural instability makes it difficult to synthesis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0014_R_d190701634e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Realizing the stability of 2H with the similar excellent electrical conductivity of the 1T phase is the main challenge and direction for great MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0015_R_d190701634e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Energy.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In previous studies, researchers have converted the 2H phase to the 1T phase through various phase change engineering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0016_R_d190701634e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Energy Chem.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Commonly used strategies are the induction of phase transitions through alkali metal intercalation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0017_R_d190701634e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater. Nano Energy.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> defect modulation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0018_R_d190701634e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> crystal phase modulation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0019_R_d190701634e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nature.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and organic molecule insertion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0020_R_d190701634e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Synth. Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, complex operating environments,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0021_R_d190701634e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> low conversion efficiencies,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0022_R_d190701634e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and unstable phase structures limit the possibility of large-scale applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0017_R_d190701634e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater. Nano Energy.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, phase transitions are now usually triggered by mild doping methods with heteroatoms (e.g., C, P, or N doping) or by introducing ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0023_R_d190701634e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Storage Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Heteroatoms can also act as electron donors to stabilize the structure of the material, while some heteroatoms are also strongly catalytic.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0024_R_d190701634e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater. ACS Nano.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the sodium storage process of MoS<sub>2</sub> is generally considered to be a reversible intercalation /extraction reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0025_R_d190701634e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Researchers typically design nanomaterials<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0026_R_d190701634e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or 3D structured channels<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0027_R_d190701634e620" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to facilitate sodium storage mechanisms. The reported studies lacked in-depth understanding of the mechanism of the sodium storage process in MoS<sub>2</sub>. Therefore, more representative studies are urgently needed to help researchers understand the sodium storage mechanism of MoS<sub>2</sub> and promote the application of 2D layer materials in sodium ion batteries for fast kinetics.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, a computational screening was first performed based on the nonmetallic elements. The results show a significant increase in the electrical conductivity of C-doped 2H-phase MoS<sub>2</sub> (2H-C). Experimental and theoretical studies show that the lattice and electronic properties of 2H-C are altered, resulting in high conductivity and lower Na ions diffusion energy barriers, which improves sodium storage capacity and storage ion kinetics. The sodium ion storage mechanism of 2H-C has been explored in depth using Density Functional Theory (DFT), Machine Learning (ML) and in situ technological tools. As a result, 2H-C is uniquely positioned to promote sodium-ion intercalation kinetics under high voltage window, and sequentially good for uniformly conversion at lower voltage. (“intercalation-conversion” mechanism). As a result, 2H-C anode material exhibits an exceptionally long cycle life of 440 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 6000 cycles, a remarkable reversible capacity of 156 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as well as superior performance at low temperatures of −10 °C. In addition, the 2H-C/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell also achieves a charge/discharge capacity of 80.0/76.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 140 cycles at 0 °C with 87% capacity retention with the power and energy densities of 187.6&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> and 217.3&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The phase modulation of the 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> anode offers valuable insights into optimizing sodium storage mechanisms in low-temperature, fast-charging sodium-ion batteries, as well as in 2D conversion-type anodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400470-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400470-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>For design the objective structure of 2H phase molybdenum disulfide (2H), an in-depth theoretical analysis was carried out through Density Functional Theory (DFT). First, a computational model for the 2H and the introduction of common non-metallic elements for doping was developed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information). The effect of doping with nonmetallic elements on the conductivity of the 2H phase was investigated by calculating the density of states for each model. The total density of states (TDOS) of 2H for semiconductor properties shows no electron density near the Fermi level (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information). And after introducing the doping of other non-metallic elements, the electron density of Carbon (C), Nitrogen (N), Phosphorus (P), Chlorine (Cl) increases at the Fermi energy level, and the TDOS values at the Fermi energy level are 9.35, 2.04, 1.82, and 1.96, respectively. The results indicate that the C-doped material has the strongest electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0028_R_d190701634e673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Environ. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the electronic conductivity of the 2H-phase MoS<sub>2</sub> (2H-C), which has been doped with C, significantly increases. This is ascribed to Mo and C atoms form impurity energy levels at the Fermi level. C doping introduces additional free electrons that could move through the material and increase the electrical conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0029_R_d190701634e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This results in a reduction of the 2H-C energy gap and an easier jump of electrons into the conduction band, which improves the electrical conductivity. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the transparent yellow regions around the atoms represent electron density, revealing how electron clouds are distributed around each atom. In the 2H-C structure, the electron cloud is located between the Mo and C atoms. This indicates a clear concentration of electrons in the region between the two nuclei, which enhances the bonding effect. In contrast to S atoms, C atoms exhibit a stronger tendency to transfer electrons to the Mo atoms. This electron transfer supports the adjustment of electron occupancy in the Mo 3<i>d</i> orbitals, ultimately leading to improved symmetry and the stabilization of the 2H-C structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0019_R_d190701634e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nature.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, through computational screening, the energy level diagram clearly shows the trend of the material conductivity (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0001">1a</a>), further distinguished the experimental study to explore the optimum C doping content. The energy gap between the HOMO and LUMO energy levels decreases significantly with increasing C doping content. This indicates that electrons are more likely to jump from occupied to unoccupied states, thus enhancing the conductivity of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0030_R_d190701634e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the amount of C doping continues to increase, the density of extra electrons in the material increases and the electrons repel each other. This causes the electron cloud to expand away from the nucleus, leading to an increase in the HOMO-LUMO energy level difference. The calculated optimum ratio is when Mo:C ≈2:1. Meanwhile, subsequent computational studies have followed this ratio. The structure of 2H-C with adsorbed Na ions was optimized (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0001">1b</a>). The 2H-C with adsorbed Na ions remained stable. The adsorption energies of Na ions at four positions in the 2H-C structure and the adsorption energies of Na ions at three adsorption sites in the 2H structure was calculated. The superiority of the 2H-C structure in terms of sodium ion storage properties is demonstrated. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0001">1c</a>, the adsorption energies of Na ions at four adsorption sites of the 2H-C structure are much larger than those of the 2H structure, and the top site of C has the largest adsorption energy for Na ions. Meanwhile, from the differential charge density plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0001">1d</a>), and Na ions show more active charge transfer in 2H-C. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0001">1e</a> shows the projected crystal orbital Hamiltonian population (pCOHP) for selected bands representative of the two structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0031_R_d190701634e721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Comput. Chem.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The integrated pCOHP (ICOHP) values below the Fermi level (<i>𝜀<sub>f</sub></i>) reflect the bonding strength.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0032_R_d190701634e731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Phys. Chem.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the Mo─C bond in 2H-C (−ICOHP = 3.42) exhibits a higher bond strength than the Mo─S bond (−ICOHP = 2.18). Theoretical calculations show that the structure is stable after doping with C. The high electronic conductivity and high Na ions adsorption energy resulting from the structural changes induced by C doping are expected to substantially improve the capacity and multiplicity performance of 2H-C in sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400470-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/95afdcf2-c3a5-4e3e-bf52-b3ed093e0a7e/aenm202400470-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/95afdcf2-c3a5-4e3e-bf52-b3ed093e0a7e/aenm202400470-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ae040431-2c67-4ba8-a698-b871c33472d9/aenm202400470-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/95afdcf2-c3a5-4e3e-bf52-b3ed093e0a7e/aenm202400470-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400470-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The values of HOMO and LUMO energy levels corresponding to different C doping contents. b) Top view of the atomic structure of 2H-C and the four adsorption sites of Na ions, as shown in S top site, C top site, Mo top site, and hollow site. c) Adsorption energy of Na ions in 2H-C and 2H. d) Difference charge density of 2H and 2H-C. e) The two models of 2H-C and 2H were analyzed by COHP and the bonding strengths of selected pairs of atoms in the two individual model structures in (d) were compared using integral values of the −ICOHP values.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Based on the simulation results mentioned above, a schematic of the preparation of 2H and 2H-C was designed and displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0002">2a</a>. First of all, ethylenediamine tri-molybdate (MoO<sub>3</sub>) was synthesized by a modified low-temperature hydrothermal method. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images showed a uniform 1D morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a>, Supporting Information). The crystal structure of MoO<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS certificate No. 35–0609) was investigated by XRD analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a>, Supporting Information). All the diffraction peaks of MoO<sub>3</sub> were consistent with those reported previously. During the carbonization process, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>-C with different C-doping concentrations were obtained depending on the treatment time. The elemental occupancies in the 2H-C materials were determined using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Normalization was also carried out to obtain 2H-C with Mo and C ratios of (3:1, 2:1, and 1:1) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The successful synthesis of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>-C was also demonstrated by XRD (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a>, Supporting Information). For comparison purposes, commercial 2H-phase molybdenum disulfide (2H-Commercial) was purchased. The XRD structure is shown in (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a>, Supporting Information). SEM clearly demonstrates the homogeneous dispersion of 2H (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0002">2b</a>), 2H-C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0002">2c</a>), MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>-C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information) and 2H-Commercial (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information). Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images displayed that the 2H exhibits a unique petal-like structure, and the 2H-C is encapsulated by the C with metallic features at the edges, suggesting that its lattice structure has been altered. In addition, high-resolution TEM images (HRTEM) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0002">2d,e</a>) show that the lattice spacing of 2H-C is ≈0.67&nbsp;nm, which is higher than that of 0.62&nbsp;nm for 2H. The results indicated that C doping changes the original lattice structure. This facilitates the enhancement of electrochemical Na ions intercalation, thus providing superior kinetic processes. Representative scanning SEM images and corresponding EDS elemental profiles confirm the homogeneous distribution of Mo, S, and C elements in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>-C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) and 2H-C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0002">2f–i</a>) samples, evidencing the homogeneous doping of elemental C.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400470-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0edc3458-c7ab-4431-9f17-27844e5bd590/aenm202400470-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0edc3458-c7ab-4431-9f17-27844e5bd590/aenm202400470-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6eedf658-2a86-4851-adee-ce8930d4eae5/aenm202400470-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0edc3458-c7ab-4431-9f17-27844e5bd590/aenm202400470-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400470-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram and ball-and-stick model for the preparation of 2H-C materials. SEM images of b) 2H and c) 2H-C. HRTEM image of d) 2H and e) 2H-C. f–i) SEM–EDS elemental mapping images of 2H-C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0003">3a</a> shows that all the diffraction peaks of 2H belong to hexagonal 2H (JCPDS certificate No. 37–1492). The (002) line emission peaks of 2H-C are shifted to a lower angle compared to the commercial bulk phase 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>. The diffraction peaks in 2H-C are changed (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0003">3a</a>), and the layer spacing of 2H-C is calculated to be 0.67&nbsp;nm according to the Bragg equation. This shift is attributed to the introduction of C into MoS<sub>2</sub> leading to a phase transition and increasing the layer spacing. In addition, the intensity of the Raman 2H-C peak decreases and shifts because the doped carbon atoms introduce new energy levels and change the positions of the original energy levels. This affects the density of electronic states of the material, which in turn affects the intensity and frequency of Raman scattering (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0003">3b</a>). Meanwhile, the electrical conductivity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0003">3c</a>) of 2H, 2H-C, and 2H-Commercial was verified by automated powder electrical conductivity testing. At a range pressure of 4–32&nbsp;MPa, the 2H-C material exhibits an electrical conductivity value close to the ultra-high value of 1T (10<sup>−2</sup> –10<sup>0</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>), which is an order of magnitude higher than that of 2H and 2H-Commercial.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0033_R_d190701634e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Nano.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> XPS were performed to determine the effect of C doping on the 2H chemical state. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the XPS analysis of both materials indicates that both 2H and 2H-C contain three intensity peaks associated with Mo 3<i>d</i>, S 2<i>p</i>, and C 1<i>s</i>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0034_R_d190701634e893" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Carbon.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the 2H-C composite contains a unique region ≈282–290&nbsp;eV, which corresponds to C 1<i>s</i>, confirming the successful incorporation of carbon into the framework of 2H. The high-resolution Mo 3<i>d</i> spectra of 2H-C were fitted to four different peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0003">3d</a>). The analysis showed that the Mo 3<i>d</i> spectra of 2H-C displayed four similar characteristic peaks at 228.7, 231.9, 288.3, and 290.4&nbsp;eV, corresponding to Mo 3<i>d<sub>3/2</sub></i>, Mo 3<i>d<sub>5/2</sub></i>, and a weak peak corresponding to Mo<sup>6+</sup> was observed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0035_R_d190701634e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Mo 3<i>d<sub>3/2</sub></i> of 2H-C was shifted. The C 1s spectrum also shows a prominent peak centered at 284.8&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0003">3e</a>). And the C1s peak in 2H-C is also shifted to the left. This is due to the fact that the doping of C may introduce additional electrons and change the electronic structure of MoS<sub>2</sub>.The left shift of the Mo 3<i>d<sub>3/2</sub></i> peak may be related to the decrease in the oxidation state of the Mo atoms, indicating that the C doping introduces a negative charge and increases the electrical conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0036_R_d190701634e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Rare Met.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the left shift of the C 1<i>s</i> peak may be related to the change of the electronic state of C atoms in MoS<sub>2</sub>, with electron transfer between C and MoS<sub>2</sub>. These changes lead to an increase in the electron density, which increased the electrical conductivity of the material. In addition, the high-resolution S 2<i>p</i> spectra of 2H-C and 2H also appeared two similar prominent peaks centered at 163.2 and 164.3&nbsp;eV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), corresponding to the S 2<i>p<sub>1/2</sub></i> and S 2<i>p<sub>3/2</sub></i> orbitals, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0028_R_d190701634e963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Environ. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In summary, when C is doped into 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, the additional electrons provided by C first occupy the dx<sup>2</sup>-y<sup>2</sup>/d<sub>xy</sub> orbitals of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0037_R_d190701634e979" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which leads to the reorganization of the Mo3<i>d</i> orbitals (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0003">3f</a>), giving it a 1T-phase nature and increasing the layer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0038_R_d190701634e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nat. Commun.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400470-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/aa89276b-4e9c-4df8-baa1-b060fe586273/aenm202400470-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/aa89276b-4e9c-4df8-baa1-b060fe586273/aenm202400470-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9a320c01-eb96-4221-ab86-c1a4bea59ecf/aenm202400470-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/aa89276b-4e9c-4df8-baa1-b060fe586273/aenm202400470-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400470-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Wide-angle XRD patterns of 2H and 2H-C. b) Raman spectrum of 2H and 2H-C. c) Powder electronic conductivity (<i>σ</i><sub>a</sub>) at 4–32&nbsp;MPa of 2H-C, 2H and 2H-Commercial. High-resolution XPS profiles of d) Mo 3<i>d</i>, e) C 1<i>s</i> of 2H and 2H-C. f) Schematic diagram of the orbital energy level mechanism of MoS<sub>2</sub> after doping with C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>During the discharge process, the CV curves of the 2H-C anode at 0.1 mV<sup>−1</sup> showed significant peak curves ≈0.8 and 1.4&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, Na ions may have different reaction mechanisms at different voltage intervals during the discharge process of 2H-C anode. To further point out the unique storage sodium mechanism of 2H-C. The unique changes of 2H-C structure during Na insertion were jointly analyzed based on the DFT principle and the ML model (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0004">4a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0039_R_d190701634e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - WIREs Comput. Mol. Sci.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Since the adsorption energy of sodium atoms intercalation in the layer skeleton is lower than the cohesion energy, at this point the sodium atoms tend to form sodium clusters and no longer contribute to the battery capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0040_R_d190701634e1044" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Phy. Rev.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The maximum number of stored ions was therefore explored based on the initial model (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0004">4b</a>) and the energy storage limit was reached when the number of inserted Na ions reached 19. Molecular dynamics simulations (AIMD) were carried out at 300 K and showed that the 2H-C material was structurally stable (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). For further analyses, the voltage changes during Na insertion were calculated and the discharge potential curves were composed of discontinuous potential points (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0004">4c</a>) (Supporting Information for details of the calculations). The results show that when the number of Na ions inserted reaches 19, the voltage drops to ≈0.1&nbsp;V, reaching the energy storage limit. Interestingly, when the number of Na ions inserted is ≈13, the voltage plummets to ≈0.8&nbsp;V. This suggests that two different ionic phases are created in the material with the insertion of Na ions. Charge values were also collected for different filling levels during Na ions insertion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The results show a clear bimodal distribution of charge. And the local environment analysis was carried out by using the Smooth Overlap of Atomic Positions (SOAP) descriptor,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0041_R_d190701634e1062" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while the 2D visualization was performed by using the MDS algorithm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0004">4d</a>). The results show that the environment during Na atoms insertion is divided into two groups. This also verifies the existence of two unique mechanisms for the 2H-C anode. In addition, the diffusion energy barrier of Na ions in the material determines the multiplicity properties of the material. By comparing the diffusion paths of Na ions in 2H and 2H-C structures, the diffusion energy barriers of Na ions in 2H and 2H-C structures were calculated, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0004">4e</a>. At longer distances, the diffusion energy barrier of the 2H-C structure (0.15&nbsp;eV) is still lower than that of 2H (0.24&nbsp;eV). This demonstrates that the 2H-C anode has excellent Na ions diffusion potential, which contributes to the fast charge/discharge efficiency of the battery.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400470-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d804b3b1-954e-4c74-88bb-218cd65122b2/aenm202400470-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d804b3b1-954e-4c74-88bb-218cd65122b2/aenm202400470-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/290bbdd8-024b-4bee-bb4e-d7aa1bf405cb/aenm202400470-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d804b3b1-954e-4c74-88bb-218cd65122b2/aenm202400470-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400470-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram of ML probing the energy storage mechanism. b) Adsorption energy profile for constant insertion of Na ions. c) Potential prediction profile for constant insertion of Na ions. d) Localized environments of sodium stored in 2H-C, with points of similar characteristics marked with similar colors. e) Migration paths of Na ions in 2H-C and diffusion energy barriers corresponding to them.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To gain insight into the storage mechanism of Na ions in 2H-C, in situ XRD measurements were carried out by applying 2H-C electrode in sodium-ion battery (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0005">5a</a>). A reversible transition of the 2H-C (002) peak is clearly observed at 14.5° due to the initial insertion of the Na ion into the 2H-C layer vacancy. Subsequently, the peak moves to a lower angle, which coincides with the lattice expansion caused by the successive entry of Na ions. During charging, the peak moves back to its original position indicated the good reversibility and stability of structure. Meanwhile, new peaks appeared at 15.2°, 17.3°, and 32.0° in the late discharge and early charging periods. The results show that Na ions are involved in the transformation reaction, produced sodium sulfides at 15.2°, 17.3°, and 32.0°.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0010_R_d190701634e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results show that the intercalation and transformation reactions are simultaneous during the discharge process, and 2H-C does not react with Na ions directly after changing the lattice structure and electronic conductivity, but reaches the transformation energy barrier only at low voltage. In contrast, the peak change in the 2H (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) anode is not significant. No significant reversible peak change was observed ≈14.5°. This is due to its small layer spacing and the inability of Na ions to insert into the interior quickly. Meanwhile, the appearance of the peak is observed ≈20°, followed by a gradual disappearance. This is because the transformation from the surface to the interior is inhomogeneous due to its semiconducting properties, which also leads to slower kinetics. In conclusion, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0005">5b</a> is a simple schematic of the 2H-C and 2H storage process. 2H-C makes it easy to intercalate Na ions into the interlayers due to its increased layer spacing, and makes Na ions uniformly distributed and fast moving due to its high electronic conductivity properties. Afterward, due to the uniform distribution of Na ions, the conversion reaction also occurs uniformly. 2H is more difficult to intercalate Na ions due to its restricted lattice structure and electronic conductivity, which makes Na ions unevenly distributed inside and outside of the 2H layer, thus leading to an uneven distribution of the conversion.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400470-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6d20b101-51f9-4e3e-9602-96cdbb18f77f/aenm202400470-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6d20b101-51f9-4e3e-9602-96cdbb18f77f/aenm202400470-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fc672cf1-2e96-4db4-bd4d-7fe02d806975/aenm202400470-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6d20b101-51f9-4e3e-9602-96cdbb18f77f/aenm202400470-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400470-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XRD maps of 2H-C and corresponding discharge/charge curve in the first cycle. b) A mechanism schematic of the 2H-C and 2H sodium storage process. In situ Nyquist plots of 2H (c) and 2H-C (d) in the first cycle. e,f) DRT distribution maps resolved from in situ EIS. g) Voltage impedance curves based on DRT fitting. The sodium-ion diffusion coefficients of 2H, 2H-C and 2H-Commercial composite in h) discharge and i) charge processes. j) Schematic of the Na storage mechanism of 2H-C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Nyquist plots before and after 2H and 2H-C-2:1 cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information) as well as linearly fitted curves in the low frequency region after cycling. The statistically fitted impedance parameters indicate (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) that the C doped material forms a more stable SEI film and enhances the electrical conductivity reduces the stress on the electrons transported through the material. To further validate the above process, in situ Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy (EIS) tests were performed on both 2H and 2H-C electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0005">5c,d</a>). During this process, the EIS change exhibits two distinct phases. As the discharge proceeded, the charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) decreased continuously from the open circuit voltage (OCV) to the fully sodiated state (0.01&nbsp;V). During the charging process, R<sub>ct</sub> shows an increasing trend, which indicates that the redox process is reversible with excellent kinetics. In order to explore the sodium storage processes of 2H and 2H-C, electrochemical processes on different time scales were analyzed in depth by means of relaxation time distributions (DRT) for in situ EIS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0005">5e,f</a>) (Supporting Information for details of the calculations). The kinetic processes are presented as different peaks in the DRT plots with time constants representing the different kinetic processes present in the impedance, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400470-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400470-bib-0042_R_d190701634e1152" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Environ. Sci.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results show that the 2H-C charging and discharging process exhibits four phases: an intercalation transformation phase during the discharging process and a reversible transition during the charging process. The voltage impedance curves were analyzed by segmental fitting of the DRT results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0005">5g</a>). The results show that 2H-C has a significant impedance reduction at 1.7–0.8&nbsp;V in the discharge stage and has a significant impedance advantage over 2H in the charging stage. This also suggests that 2H-C exhibits a significant Na ions intercalation-conversion advantage at a unique voltage phase.</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information) shows a typical constant current intermittent titration technique (GITT) test procedure. The complete GITT curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information) and Na ions diffusion kinetics were evaluated by calculating the Na ions diffusion coefficients (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0005">5h,i</a>) of the three materials during charging and discharging. During the initial discharge phase, the 2H-C anode did not show a significant ion diffusion advantage. This may be due to the limitation of the internal electron and transport capacity of the material at high voltage, which also leads to the slowing down of the Na ion intercalation efficiency. Subsequently, the 2H-C exhibits fast Na ion diffusion coefficients during both the 1.7–0.8&nbsp;V discharge and charging processes. This shows that the 2H-C anode has excellent energy storage advantages in a unique voltage interval and maintains good electrochemical reversibility. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0005">5j</a> summarizes the insights into the sodium storage mechanism in 2H-C. Due to the unique regulatory mechanism of C, a larger interlayer spacing lattice structure and faster electronic conductivity are produced. While storing sodium ions, the regulated structure of 2H-C exhibits a process that promotes ion intercalation under a high voltage window of 1.7–0.8&nbsp;V and further uniform conversion and sustained sodium storage at lower voltages at a later stage.</p>
         
         <p>Furthermore, the superiority of the 2H-C structure in improving sodium ion storage kinetics and performance under extreme condition was investigated. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0006">6a</a> shows the cycling performance of SIBs assembled with 2H, 2H-commercial, and 2H-C (with different C doping concentrations) at a current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The initial capacity of 2H-C-2:1 is 504 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is much higher than that of 2H (395 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and 2H-commercial (346 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). 2H-C-2:1 remained at 486 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles. This suggests that the 2H-C structure can change the crystal structure and expand the layer spacing to obtain excellent sodium ion storage capacity. The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) of 2H and 2H-C-2:1 anode at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a,b</a> (Supporting Information). During cycling, the first coulombic efficiencies (ICE) during cycling were 91.7% and 92.1% for 2H and 2H-C, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16c</a>, Supporting Information). As the number of cycles increases, the 2H-C-2:1 electrode has higher reversible capacity and cycling performance compared to 2H. This is mainly attributed to the increased layer spacing and better charge transfer due to C doping. To validate the fast kinetics of 2H-C structure while storage sodium ions, 2H-C-2:1, 2H, and 2H-Commercial were applied in low temperatures and fast-charging states (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0006">6b</a>). The performance indicated that 2H-C performed the higher capacity and stability at a range temperature from 30 to −10 °C under 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. At an initial temperature of 30 °C, 2H-C shows an excellent capacity of 551 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, much higher than the 437 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of 2H and 2H-Commercial. As the temperature was gradually lowered to −10 °C, 2H-C consistently maintained its excellent performance at 20 °C (469 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), 10 °C (423 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), 0 °C (380 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and −10 °C (257 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), respectively. The GCD curves of 2H-C-2:1 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information) at different temperatures also indicates its robust rate performance and low polarization. On the other hand, the 2H and 2H-Commercial showed capacity degradation at 10 °C and complete failure at 0 °C, respectively. Compared with 2H and 2H-Commercial for fast-charging, 2H-C-2:1 exhibited the best rate capability at current densities ranging from 0.2 to 100.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0006">6c</a>). Corresponding GCD curves were also performed for 2H (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16d</a>, Supporting Information) and 2H-C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16e</a>, Supporting Information) at different current densities. Its capacity was 570, 517, 487, 466, 429, 393, 352, and 258 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0, 20.0, and 50.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at a current density of 100.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 2H-C-2:1 anode still maintains a high discharge specific capacity of 156 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The difference between 2H-C-2:1 and 2H and 2H-Commercial becomes apparent with increasing current density. 2H completely loses its sodium storage performance at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, while 2H-Commercial has almost no sodium storage performance after 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. This is due to the excellent electronic conductivity brought about by C doping, as well as its special “intercalation-conversion” storage mechanism, which brings about rapid kinetic effects. In order to reflect the advantages of this work in terms of electrochemical properties, atomic structure, energy storage advantages and surface loading, a detailed comparison with similar sulfide studies in recent years is compared (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Also comparing with similar work at 0–100 C current density, the present study still has advantages in some ranges (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0006">6d</a>). To further understand the electrochemical kinetics of Na ions in 2H, 2H-C-2:1, and 2H-Commercial anodes, CV curves were analyzed at different scan rates from 0.1 to 5.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and for the first five turns at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The pseudocapacitance contributions were also calculated for the 2H, 2H-C-2:1, and 2H-Commercial (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information) anodes at 0.1 to 5.0 mV<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The results indicate that the 2H-C electrode capacitance contribution is significantly higher than that of 2H and 2H-Commercial. This suggests that the C doped 2H-C structure promotes the redox reactions displayed by the electrodes, thereby improving transport kinetics and charge storage rate capabilities. In addition, the long cycle life of 2H-C-2:1 electrode was tested at a high current density of 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0006">6e</a>). As expected, the 2H-C-2:1 electrode produced a reversible capacity of 440 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 6000 cycles in SIBs, which ascribed to the structure stability of 2H-C.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400470-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9ea3cbd7-5508-4d4e-a710-20e1ed6857a0/aenm202400470-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9ea3cbd7-5508-4d4e-a710-20e1ed6857a0/aenm202400470-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/72905b9e-6599-4c70-95e5-42b25713689c/aenm202400470-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9ea3cbd7-5508-4d4e-a710-20e1ed6857a0/aenm202400470-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400470-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Comparison of cycling performance of 2H, 2H-C with different C doping ratios and 2H-Commercial at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Cycling performance of 2H, 2H-C-2:1, and 2H-Commercial under a range temperature from 30 to −10 °C. c) Rate performance of 2H, 2H-C-2:1, and 2H-Commercial at a range current density of 0.2–100.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Comparison of multiplier performance of this work with other similar work. e) The long cycle performance of 2H-C-2:1 at a current density of 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. f) Cycling performance of a full cell at 0 °C. g) The 100th discharge profile of the full-cell at 1C (110&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) according to the cycling test. h) Schematic of the pouch full-cell structure and SEM and optical image of the LED powered using the pouch full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further evaluate the 2H-C-2:1 anode for fast charging applications in low-temperature SIBs, the full cells using Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C as cathode and 2H-C-2:1 as anode was assembled. The cycling performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C was first tested at 1C (110&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). The results showed that after 50 cycles, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C still maintained the excellent performance of 76.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the charging and discharging curves of the 1st, 10th, and 50th cycles also showed the electrochemical stability of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20b</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, based on the charge/discharge curves of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C and 2H-C-2:1 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), the charge/discharge potential difference between Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C and 2H-C-2:1 is larger, which indicates that the electrochemical kinetics has been improved. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0006">6f</a>, the full cell was tested at a low temperature 0 °C, which provides a long-lasting charge/discharge capacity of 80.0/76.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with 87% capacity retention after 140 cycles under 1C. Notably, the complete battery was then tested at −10 °C. The charging and discharging capacities after 50 cycles were 74.2/73.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with 85% capacity retention (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). Calculated from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0006">6g</a> and (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23a–d</a>, Supporting Information), the power and energy densities of full cell were 187.6&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> and 217.3&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, respectively under 0 °C. A high volumetric capacity of 45.7 Ah L<sup>−1</sup> was also maintained after 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23e</a>, Supporting Information). Finally, based on the fast-charging performance of the 2H-C anode full cell, the pouch full-cell was successfully assembled to power a light-emitting diode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400470-fig-0006">6h</a>), which further demonstrates the great potential of the 2H-C anode for fast charging SIBs at low temperatures. In conclusion, the 2H-C structure promotes the fast kinetic behavior of Na ions storage because of the unique “intercalation-conversion” mechanism, and delivered superior performance at extreme condition such as low-temperature and fast-charging.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400470-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400470-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this study, a stable C doped MoS<sub>2</sub> structure with high electronic conductivity was successfully screened using the DFT method and synthesized experimentally. In addition, comprehensive analyses using ML, DFT and in situ techniques have demonstrated a more stable structure, revealing a unique intercalation-promoting and conversion-uniformly mechanism for sodium ions storage for 2H-C anode, which facilitates fast kinetic. Therefore, the 2H-C anode has superior sodium storage performance under a wide temperature range from 30 to −10 °C, fast-charging performance (156 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> capacity at 100.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and long cycling stability (440 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for 6000 cycles at 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) when applied in SIBs. Additionally, 2H-C/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell also performed charge/discharge capacity of 80.0/76.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with 87% capacity retention after 140 cycles at 0 °C with the power and energy densities of 187.6&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> and 217.3&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Based on the unique “intercalation-conversion” mechanism, this study opens up new research avenues for the application of 2D conversion-type anode in low-temperature and fast-charging SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400470-sec-0040-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
